  - "The only purpose for which power can be rightfully exercised over any member of a civilized community, against his will, is to prevent harm to others"
  - Applies to both legal coercion and social pressure ("tyranny of the majority")
  - Rules out paternalism—can't coerce people "for their own good"
  - Rules out moralism—can't coerce people just because we disapprove
  - Freedom of thought, expression, and lifestyle essential to human flourishing
- **Empiricism in logic:** All knowledge derives from experience
  - Even logic and mathematics are generalizations from experience
  - No a priori knowledge; radical empiricism
  - Induction is the foundation of all inference
  - *System of Logic* (1843): comprehensive account of scientific method
- **Feminism:** Early systematic case for women's equality
  - *The Subjection of Women* (1869): legal status of women compared to slavery
  - Marriage should be a partnership of equals
  - Women entitled to education, employment, political participation
  - Collaborated extensively with Harriet Taylor Mill
  - Advocated for women's suffrage in Parliament
- **Individuality and self-development:** Human flourishing requires freedom
  - Conformity stunts human development
  - Eccentricity and experiments in living are valuable
  - Truth emerges from free discussion and competing ideas
  - Silencing any opinion assumes infallibility

**Key Works:**
- *A System of Logic* (1843) — comprehensive treatise on inductive reasoning and scientific method
- *On Liberty* (1859) — classic statement of liberal political philosophy
- *Utilitarianism* (1861) — refined defense of utilitarian ethics
- *The Subjection of Women* (1869) — pioneering feminist philosophy
- *Autobiography* (1873) — influential account of his education and intellectual development
- *Considerations on Representative Government* (1861) — democratic theory

**Connections:**
- *Influenced by*: Jeremy Bentham (godfather), James Mill (father), Harriet Taylor Mill (wife), Wordsworth (recovery from crisis)
- *Influences*: Liberal political tradition, analytic philosophy, feminist thought
- *Opposed to*: Intuitionism, conservatism, paternalism
- *Anticipated*: Preference utilitarianism, rule utilitarianism debates

**Common Misconceptions:**
- Mill's utilitarianism isn't crude hedonism—quality of pleasure matters, not just quantity
- The harm principle isn't libertarian—Mill allowed regulation for positive social goals
- He wasn't anti-religious—but opposed religious imposition on others
- His feminism was radical for his time, though limited by today's standards
- The "mental crisis" wasn't a breakdown—it was philosophical reorientation

**Why He Matters:** Mill was the most influential English-speaking philosopher of the 19th century. His *On Liberty* remains the foundational text of liberal political philosophy. His refinement of utilitarianism addressed crude hedonism's flaws. His *Subjection of Women* pioneered philosophical feminism. His empiricist logic influenced philosophy of science for generations.

**Future Exploration:**
- Does the distinction between higher and lower pleasures undermine hedonism?
- How do we identify "competent judges"?
- Is the harm principle determinate enough to guide policy?
- What's the relationship between Mill's liberalism and his utilitarianism?
- How did Harriet Taylor influence Mill's thought?

---

### American Pragmatism

> *Ideas as instruments; truth as what works*

Pragmatism, America's distinctive contribution to philosophy, emerged in the 1870s and became a major force in 20th-century thought. Rejecting the quest for certainty and correspondence theories of truth, pragmatists focused on the practical consequences of ideas. They shared anti-foundationalism, fallibilism, and an emphasis on experience and inquiry, though they developed these themes in different directions.

#### Charles Sanders Peirce (1839 – 1914)

**Core Problems:** Logic, epistemology (nature of inquiry), semiotics (theory of signs), metaphysics, philosophy of science

**Central Ideas:**
- **The pragmatic maxim:** Meaning lies in practical effects
  - "Consider what effects, which might conceivably have practical bearings, we conceive the object of our conception to have. Then, our conception of these effects is the whole of our conception of the object."
  - To understand "hard," consider its practical effects: scratching other things, resisting pressure
  - Clarifies meaning; eliminates meaningless metaphysical disputes
  - Later called his view "pragmaticism" to distinguish from James's version
- **Beliefs as habits of action:** Belief guides conduct
  - Doubt is an irritation that inquiry aims to settle
  - Belief is a stable habit of action—rules we apply to solve problems
  - Thought's function is to move from doubt to belief
  - Four methods of fixing belief: tenacity, authority, a priori, scientific inquiry
  - Only scientific method is genuinely self-correcting
- **Fallibilism:** All knowledge is provisional
  - No beliefs are absolutely certain; any may be revised
  - Anti-foundationalist: no indubitable starting points
  - Yet not skeptical: genuine questions can in principle be answered
  - Science is self-correcting community of inquirers
  - Truth is what inquiry would converge on "in the long run"
- **Semiotics:** Theory of signs
  - "All this universe is perfused with signs"
  - Three types: icons (resemble referent), indices (causally connected), symbols (conventional)
  - Logic is "formal semiotic"—study of signs and inference
  - Thought itself is a sign process
- **Abduction:** Creative hypothesis formation
  - Three modes of inference: deduction, induction, abduction
  - Abduction: generating hypotheses to explain surprising facts
  - "The only kind of reasoning that introduces new ideas"
  - First stage of scientific inquiry

**Key Works:**
- "The Fixation of Belief" (1877) — methods of settling opinion
- "How to Make Our Ideas Clear" (1878) — the pragmatic maxim
- "A Guess at the Riddle" (1887–88) — metaphysical categories
- Collected Papers (posthumous, 8 volumes) — assembled from manuscripts

**Connections:**
- *Influenced by*: Kant, Scottish common sense philosophy, scientific practice
- *Influences*: William James, John Dewey, semiotics, philosophy of science
- *Opposed to*: Cartesian foundationalism, nominalism, psychologism
- *Anticipated*: Popper's fallibilism, Kuhn's scientific communities, Quine's holism

**Common Misconceptions:**
- Peirce isn't saying truth is "whatever works for me"—truth is long-run convergence of inquiry
- "Pragmaticism" was coined because James had "kidnapped" pragmatism
- He was primarily a logician and scientist, not just a philosopher
- His obscurity in his lifetime was due to personal difficulties, not lack of brilliance
- Fallibilism isn't skepticism—we can have well-grounded beliefs

**Why He Matters:** Peirce founded pragmatism and made foundational contributions to logic, semiotics, and philosophy of science. His pragmatic maxim transformed how philosophers think about meaning. His fallibilism and theory of inquiry influenced virtually all subsequent American philosophy. His semiotics became a major field. He anticipated many developments in 20th-century philosophy of science.

**Future Exploration:**
- What's the relationship between Peirce's and James's pragmatisms?
- Does fallibilism undermine the possibility of knowledge?
- Is abduction a legitimate mode of inference?
- How does inquiry converge on truth "in the long run"?
- What's the relationship between semiotics and logic?

---

#### William James (1842 – 1910)

**Core Problems:** Epistemology (nature of truth), philosophy of religion, metaphysics (mind-body), psychology

**Central Ideas:**
- **Pragmatic theory of truth:** Truth as what works
  - True ideas are those that we can "validate," "verify," "corroborate"
  - "The true is only the expedient in the way of thinking"
  - Truth is made, not found—ideas become true as they prove useful
  - More individualistic and action-oriented than Peirce's version
  - Truth is "the cash-value" of an idea
- **The will to believe:** Justified faith beyond evidence
  - In "genuine options" (live, forced, momentous), belief without conclusive evidence is legitimate
  - We can't always wait for evidence; some choices must be made now
  - Religious faith can be rationally held even without proof
  - Not irrationalism—there are constraints on when belief is justified
  - "Our passional nature not only lawfully may, but must, decide"
- **Radical empiricism:** Experience as fundamental reality
  - Pure experience is the basic "stuff" of reality
  - Neither purely mental nor purely physical—prior to that distinction
  - Relations are experienced, not just terms (against British empiricism)
  - "Neutral monism": mind and matter are arrangements of experience
  - Consciousness is a function, not a thing
- **Pluralism:** Reality is irreducibly many
  - Against monism (Hegel, Royce): the universe is not one unified whole
  - Reality is a "multiverse," not a "universe"
  - Allows for genuine novelty, freedom, and chance
  - "Meliorism": world can get better if we work at it (between optimism and pessimism)
- **Psychology of religion:** Empirical study of religious experience
  - *Varieties of Religious Experience*: scientific study of mysticism, conversion, sainthood
  - Focus on individual experience, not institutions or dogma
  - "Fruits, not roots"—judge religion by its effects on life
  - Defends religion's value while remaining philosophically open

**Key Works:**
- *The Principles of Psychology* (1890) — founding text of American psychology
- *The Will to Believe* (1897) — essays on faith and evidence
- *The Varieties of Religious Experience* (1902) — psychology of religion
- *Pragmatism* (1907) — popular statement of pragmatic philosophy
- *A Pluralistic Universe* (1909) — metaphysical pluralism
- *Essays in Radical Empiricism* (1912, posthumous) — neutral monism

**Connections:**
- *Influenced by*: Peirce (pragmatism), Renouvier (free will), Darwin
- *Influences*: John Dewey, Bertrand Russell (debates), phenomenology, psychology
- *Opposed to*: Absolute idealism, determinism, religious dogmatism
- *Contemporary with*: Royce (friend and opponent), Bergson (ally)

**Common Misconceptions:**
- "Whatever works" isn't James's view—usefulness must be cashed out in experience
- The will to believe isn't believing whatever you want—strict conditions apply
- He wasn't anti-intellectual—he valued reason but saw its limits
- Radical empiricism isn't subjectivist—experience is neutral between mental and physical
- His pluralism wasn't relativism—he believed in objective inquiry

**Why He Matters:** James made philosophy accessible and relevant to life. His *Principles of Psychology* helped found American psychology as a discipline. His pragmatic theory of truth, while controversial, shifted epistemological debates. His *Varieties of Religious Experience* pioneered the empirical study of religion. His pluralism and meliorism offered alternatives to both determinism and naive optimism.

**Future Exploration:**
- Is the pragmatic theory of truth coherent, or circular?
- When exactly is the "will to believe" legitimate?
- What's the relationship between radical empiricism and phenomenology?
- Can pluralism be maintained without collapsing into relativism?
- How does James's psychology inform his philosophy?

---

#### John Dewey (1859 – 1952)

**Core Problems:** Epistemology (nature of inquiry), ethics, political philosophy (democracy), philosophy of education, aesthetics

**Central Ideas:**
- **Instrumentalism:** Ideas as tools for problem-solving
  - Knowledge results from "inquiry"—active problem-solving in response to environmental challenges
  - Ideas are instruments for reorganizing experience, not mirrors of reality
  - "Knowing is literally something which we do"
  - Reject the "spectator theory of knowledge"—we're participants, not observers
  - Truth is warranted assertibility—what inquiry establishes
- **Experience:** The transaction between organism and environment
  - Against the subjective/objective split: experience is the ongoing interaction
  - "Experience-nature transactions" replace mind/world dualism
  - Experience is active, not passive reception of data
  - Continuity between ordinary experience and scientific inquiry
  - Art and aesthetic experience reveal experience's fulfilling possibilities
- **Democracy as a way of life:** Not just a political system
  - Democracy is social cooperation, shared inquiry, collective problem-solving
  - "Democracy and the one, ultimate, ethical ideal of humanity are... synonymous"
  - Requires conditions for free communication and associated living
  - Schools are democracy's laboratories—where democratic habits form
  - Intelligence applied to social problems
- **Progressive education:** Learning through experience
  - Against rote learning and passive absorption
  - "Learning by doing"—active engagement with problems
  - Education should develop capacities for observation, judgment, foresight
  - Students as active inquirers, not recipients of fixed knowledge
  - Curriculum should connect to students' experience and interests
  - *Democracy and Education* (1916): education is continuous reconstruction of experience
- **Naturalism:** Continuity of human and natural
  - Philosophy should be continuous with science
  - Values emerge from natural processes—no supernatural foundations needed
  - Growth is the only moral end: continuous expansion of experience
  - Reject fixed ends—means and ends are continuous

**Key Works:**
- *How We Think* (1910) — theory of reflective inquiry
- *Democracy and Education* (1916) — philosophy of education and democracy
- *Reconstruction in Philosophy* (1920) — critique and reform of philosophy
- *Experience and Nature* (1925) — metaphysics of experience
- *The Quest for Certainty* (1929) — critique of epistemological foundationalism
- *Art as Experience* (1934) — aesthetics and theory of experience
- *Logic: The Theory of Inquiry* (1938) — comprehensive theory of inquiry

**Connections:**
- *Influenced by*: Hegel (via T.H. Green), Peirce, James, Darwin
- *Influences*: Progressive education, American liberalism, social philosophy
- *Opposed to*: Fixed dualisms, foundationalism, spectator theory
- *Public intellectual*: Active in political causes, education reform

**Common Misconceptions:**
- Progressive education isn't "anything goes"—Dewey emphasized structured inquiry
- Instrumentalism isn't anti-theoretical—theory is a tool for guiding practice
- He wasn't a relativist—inquiry can yield genuine knowledge
- His politics weren't naive—he engaged seriously with critics of liberalism
- Experience isn't subjective—it's the organism-environment transaction

**Why He Matters:** Dewey was America's public philosopher for half a century. His instrumentalism offered a distinctive pragmatist epistemology. His philosophy of education transformed pedagogical practice worldwide. His democratic theory connected politics, education, and inquiry. He showed how philosophy could engage with real social problems while maintaining rigor.

**Future Exploration:**
- Is "warranted assertibility" the same as truth?
- How does Dewey's democracy differ from liberal proceduralism?
- Is progressive education effective? What went wrong with its implementation?
- How does aesthetic experience relate to everyday experience?
- Can Dewey's naturalism ground genuine normativity?

---

## 20th Century & Contemporary Western

> *The great divide: analysis and existence*

### Analytic Tradition

> *Clarity, logic, and the analysis of language*

The analytic tradition emerged in the early 20th century as a revolt against idealism, emphasizing logical rigor, linguistic analysis, and piecemeal problem-solving over grand system-building. Beginning with Frege's work in logic and the Cambridge philosophers Russell and Moore, it became the dominant style in Anglophone philosophy.

#### Gottlob Frege (1848 – 1925)

**Core Problems:** Philosophy of logic, philosophy of mathematics, philosophy of language

**Central Ideas:**
- **Modern predicate logic:** Revolutionized logic itself
  - *Begriffsschrift* (1879): invented quantified variables and predicate calculus
  - Freed logic from Aristotelian subject-predicate form
  - First fully axiomatic logical system
  - Made possible rigorous treatment of mathematical reasoning
  - Foundation for all subsequent formal logic
- **Logicism:** Mathematics is reducible to logic
  - Arithmetic truths are logical truths, not synthetic a priori (contra Kant)
  - Numbers are logical objects, definable in purely logical terms
  - *Grundlagen der Arithmetik* (1884): philosophical foundations
  - *Grundgesetze der Arithmetik* (1893, 1903): formal development
  - Program undermined by Russell's Paradox (in Basic Law V)
- **Sense and reference (*Sinn* and *Bedeutung*):** Two aspects of meaning
  - Reference: the object a term picks out ("the Morning Star" = Venus)
  - Sense: the mode of presentation, the "cognitive value"
  - "Morning Star" and "Evening Star" have same reference, different sense
  - Explains why "a = a" and "a = b" differ cognitively
  - Foundational for philosophy of language
- **Compositionality:** Meaning is built up systematically
  - The meaning of a sentence depends on meanings of parts plus structure
  - The "Frege-Russell" tradition in semantics
  - Function-argument structure replaces subject-predicate

**Key Works:**
- *Begriffsschrift* (1879) — invention of modern logic
- *Die Grundlagen der Arithmetik* (1884) — philosophical foundations of arithmetic
- "Über Sinn und Bedeutung" (1892) — sense and reference
- *Grundgesetze der Arithmetik* (1893, 1903) — formal logicist program

**Connections:**
- *Influenced*: Russell, Wittgenstein, Carnap, all analytic philosophy
- *Influences on him*: Leibniz (logical calculus), Kant (apriorism, then rejected)
- *Russell's Paradox*: Devastating critique of Basic Law V

**Common Misconceptions:**
- Frege wasn't primarily a philosopher—he was a mathematician who did philosophy
- The sense/reference distinction isn't about words vs. things—both are semantic
- Logicism's "failure" doesn't diminish Frege's achievement in logic itself
- He wasn't recognized in his lifetime—died in obscurity, anti-Semitic views emerged late

**Why He Matters:** Frege virtually invented modern logic, the most significant advance since Aristotle. His philosophy of language (sense/reference, compositionality) shaped analytic philosophy. Though logicism faced problems, the program inspired all subsequent philosophy of mathematics.

**Future Exploration:**
- Can logicism be rescued (neo-Fregeanism)?
- Is the sense/reference distinction tenable?
- How do Frege's views relate to contemporary semantics?

---

#### Bertrand Russell (1872 – 1970)

**Core Problems:** Philosophy of logic, philosophy of mathematics, epistemology, metaphysics, philosophy of language

**Central Ideas:**
- **Logical atomism:** Reality consists of independent atomic facts
  - The world is a plurality of facts, not things
  - Complex facts are built from simple ones
  - Language, ideally, should mirror this structure
  - Analysis reveals the logical form hidden by ordinary grammar
  - Influenced by Wittgenstein (mutual influence)
- **Theory of descriptions:** Definite descriptions aren't names
  - "The present King of France is bald"—how can this be meaningful if there's no king?
  - Solution: "The" is not a referring expression but a quantifier
  - The sentence asserts existence, uniqueness, and predication
  - Reveals logical form distinct from grammatical form
  - "On Denoting" (1905): paradigm of analytic philosophy
- **Russell's Paradox:** Contradiction in naive set theory
  - Consider the set of all sets that don't contain themselves
  - Does it contain itself? Either answer leads to contradiction
  - Undermined Frege's logicist program
  - Led to type theory as a solution
- **Type theory:** Hierarchy to avoid paradoxes
  - Objects, sets of objects, sets of sets... form distinct types
  - Prevents self-referential paradoxes
  - *Principia Mathematica* (with Whitehead): monumental logicist work
- **Knowledge by acquaintance and description:** Two ways of knowing
  - Acquaintance: direct awareness (sense-data, universals, self)
  - Description: knowing *that* something satisfies a description
  - Most of our knowledge is by description

**Key Works:**
- *The Principles of Mathematics* (1903) — early logicism
- "On Denoting" (1905) — theory of descriptions
- *Principia Mathematica* (with Whitehead, 1910–13) — logicist magnum opus
- "The Philosophy of Logical Atomism" (1918) — metaphysical lectures
- *The Problems of Philosophy* (1912) — accessible introduction
- *A History of Western Philosophy* (1945) — popular survey

**Connections:**
- *Influenced by*: Frege, Moore (rebellion against idealism), Peano
- *Influences*: Wittgenstein (teacher and colleague), logical positivism, analytic philosophy
- *Public intellectual*: Peace activism, Nobel Prize in Literature (1950)

**Common Misconceptions:**
- Russell's Paradox didn't "destroy" Frege—it revealed a specific problem
- Logical atomism was a phase—Russell changed views repeatedly
- *Principia* didn't "reduce" math to logic—it required non-logical axioms
- He wasn't just a logician—major work in epistemology, ethics, politics

**Why He Matters:** Russell (with Moore) founded analytic philosophy. His theory of descriptions showed how logical analysis reveals hidden structure. *Principia Mathematica* was a landmark in logic. His paradox transformed set theory and foundations of mathematics. He made philosophy accessible to general audiences.

**Future Exploration:**
- Is the theory of descriptions correct?
- What's the relationship between logical and metaphysical atomism?
- Did logicism ultimately succeed or fail?
- How did Russell's philosophy evolve over his long career?

---

#### G.E. Moore (1873 – 1958)

**Core Problems:** Ethics (metaethics), epistemology, metaphysics

**Central Ideas:**
- **The naturalistic fallacy:** "Good" cannot be defined in natural terms
  - Any definition of "good" (pleasure, evolutionary fitness, God's will) commits this fallacy
  - "Good" is simple, unanalyzable—like "yellow"
  - You can't derive "ought" from "is" (though Moore's point is different)
  - Foundation of non-naturalist moral realism
- **The Open Question Argument:** Test for naturalistic fallacy
  - For any proposed definition of "good" (e.g., "good = pleasurable")
  - It remains an open question: "X is pleasurable, but is it good?"
  - If definition were correct, question would be trivially answered
  - Therefore "good" is not identical to any natural property
- **Ethical intuitionism:** We know basic moral truths directly
  - "Good" is known by intuition, not inference or experience
  - Moral knowledge is non-inferential
  - We just "see" that some things are good
  - Influenced by ideal utilitarianism (good states of affairs)
- **Common sense philosophy:** Defense of ordinary beliefs
  - Against skepticism: "Here is one hand, here is another"
  - Common sense beliefs are more certain than philosophical premises that deny them
  - Burden of proof on skeptic, not on common sense
  - "A Defence of Common Sense" (1925)
- **The method of analysis:** Clarifying concepts and propositions
  - Philosophy's task is to analyze and clarify, not to discover new facts
  - Careful attention to what we actually mean
  - Influenced analytic philosophy's style

**Key Works:**
- *Principia Ethica* (1903) — naturalistic fallacy, open question argument
- "A Defence of Common Sense" (1925) — against skepticism
- "Proof of an External World" (1939) — common sense realism
- *Some Main Problems of Philosophy* (1953) — lectures on metaphysics

**Connections:**
- *With*: Russell (co-founder of analytic philosophy, Cambridge colleagues)
- *Influenced*: Wittgenstein, ordinary language philosophy, metaethics
- *Against*: British idealism (Bradley, McTaggart), ethical naturalism

**Common Misconceptions:**
- The naturalistic fallacy isn't the is-ought fallacy (though related)
- Moore wasn't a utilitarian—he rejected hedonism but valued good states of affairs
- His common sense defense isn't naive—it's a methodological claim about certainty
- The open question argument may beg the question (Frankena's objection)

**Why He Matters:** Moore's *Principia Ethica* transformed 20th-century ethics, establishing the autonomy of ethics and the agenda of metaethics. His rebellion against idealism (with Russell) launched analytic philosophy. His common sense philosophy influenced later ordinary language approaches.

**Future Exploration:**
- Does the open question argument succeed?
- Can non-naturalism explain moral knowledge?
- What's the relationship between analysis and common sense?
- How does Moore's intuition relate to moral epistemology?

---

#### Ludwig Wittgenstein (1889 – 1951)

**Core Problems:** Logic, philosophy of language, philosophy of mind, philosophy of mathematics

**Central Ideas (Early—*Tractatus Logico-Philosophicus*, 1921):**
- **Picture theory of meaning:** Propositions picture facts
  - Language represents reality by sharing logical form with it
  - Elementary propositions picture atomic facts
  - Complex propositions are truth-functions of elementary ones
  - What can be said can be said clearly; rest is silence
- **Logical atomism:** World consists of atomic facts
  - Facts, not things, are the ultimate constituents
  - Objects combine in atomic facts (states of affairs)
  - Influenced by and influenced Russell
- **The limits of language:** Whereof one cannot speak...
  - Ethics, aesthetics, the mystical—transcend what language can express
  - Philosophy's task: to show the limits of the sayable
  - "My propositions are elucidatory... he who understands me finally recognizes them as senseless"
  - Therapeutic conception: once limits seen, philosophical problems dissolve

**Central Ideas (Later—*Philosophical Investigations*, published 1953):**
- **Language games:** Language is activity embedded in practice
  - Meaning is use—how words function in practice
  - Different uses = different "games" with different rules
  - No single essence of language; family resemblances instead
  - Rejected the picture theory; language does more than represent
- **Family resemblance:** Concepts without common essence
  - What do all "games" have in common? Nothing!
  - Overlapping similarities, like family members
  - Against Platonic essences and definitions
- **Rule-following and private language:** No private language is possible
  - Following a rule is a public practice, not a private mental act
  - There can be no language whose words refer to private sensations only
  - Because: no distinction between seeming right and being right
  - Devastating critique of Cartesian privacy
- **Philosophical method:** Therapy, not theory
  - Philosophy describes but doesn't explain
  - Dissolve problems by showing how language misleads
  - "Philosophy is a battle against the bewitchment of our intelligence by language"
  - No theses in philosophy—just reminders of what we already know

**Key Works:**
- *Tractatus Logico-Philosophicus* (1921) — early systematic work
- *Philosophical Investigations* (1953) — later anti-systematic work
- *On Certainty* (1969) — epistemology, certainty, and doubt
- *Remarks on the Foundations of Mathematics* (1956) — philosophy of math

**Connections:**
- *Influenced by*: Frege, Russell, Schopenhauer, Tolstoy
- *Early influences*: Logical positivism (though they misread him)
- *Later influences*: Ordinary language philosophy, Quine, Kripke, philosophy of mind
- *Unique figure*: Repudiated his own early work

**Common Misconceptions:**
- The "two Wittgensteins" are genuinely different, but continuities exist (therapeutic aim)
- The *Tractatus* isn't positivist—the "mystical" is crucial
- "Meaning is use" isn't a slogan—it's a methodological reminder
- Private language argument isn't about *secret* language—it's about *necessarily* private language
- He didn't say philosophy was meaningless—philosophical *problems* are confusions

**Why He Matters:** Wittgenstein may be the most influential philosopher of the 20th century, remarkably through two quite different philosophies. The *Tractatus* shaped logical positivism and philosophy of logic. The *Investigations* transformed philosophy of language, mind, and philosophical methodology. His anti-theoretical, therapeutic approach remains controversial and influential.

**Future Exploration:**
- Is there continuity between early and late Wittgenstein?
- Does the private language argument succeed?
- What is the relationship between rule-following and meaning?
- Is the therapeutic conception of philosophy viable?
- How does Wittgenstein's philosophy of mathematics differ from logicism?

---

#### The Logical Positivists: Rudolf Carnap (1891 – 1970) & A.J. Ayer (1910 – 1989)

**Core Problems:** Epistemology, philosophy of science, philosophy of language, metaphysics (its elimination)

**Central Ideas:**
- **The verification principle:** Meaning requires verifiability
  - A statement is meaningful only if empirically verifiable
  - Metaphysical statements (God, the Absolute, Being) are meaningless—not false, but nonsense
  - Strong version: conclusive verification required
  - Weak version (Ayer): evidence can render statement probable
  - Criterion of demarcation between science and metaphysics
- **The analytic-synthetic distinction:** Two kinds of truths
  - Analytic: true by meaning/definition (logic, mathematics)
  - Synthetic: true by empirical fact
  - No synthetic a priori (contra Kant)
  - All meaningful statements are one or the other
- **Logical construction of the world:** Carnap's project
  - *Aufbau* (1928): reconstruct scientific concepts from sense experience
  - Reduction of all concepts to a base of immediate experience
  - Logical syntax as the method of philosophy
  - Later shifted to semantic approach
- **Emotivism in ethics:** Moral statements express attitudes
  - "X is good" doesn't state a fact but expresses approval
  - Ayer: ethical statements are ejaculations, like "Boo!" and "Hurrah!"
  - No moral knowledge—ethics isn't cognitive
  - Extends verification principle to ethics
- **Unity of science:** All sciences share one method
  - Physics as model; other sciences reducible in principle
  - Unified language of science
  - Vienna Circle collaboration

**Key Works:**
- Carnap: *Der logische Aufbau der Welt* (1928), "The Elimination of Metaphysics" (1932), *Logical Syntax of Language* (1934)
- Ayer: *Language, Truth and Logic* (1936) — positivism for English readers

**Connections:**
- *Vienna Circle*: Schlick, Neurath, Gödel (mathematics), Hempel
- *Influenced by*: Frege, Russell, Wittgenstein (*Tractatus*), Mach
- *Criticized by*: Quine ("Two Dogmas"), Popper (falsificationism), later Wittgenstein
- *Legacy*: Philosophy of science, formal semantics

**Common Misconceptions:**
- Positivism isn't "positive" or optimistic—it's about what's positively knowable
- The *Tractatus* influenced them, but Wittgenstein rejected their reading
- Verification principle is itself unverifiable—they knew this was a problem
- They weren't simply anti-philosophy—they practiced rigorous philosophical analysis
- Carnap evolved considerably—later work more nuanced than early "eliminations"

**Why They Matter:** The positivists professionalized philosophy of science and clarified the logic of scientific reasoning. Their verification principle, though ultimately rejected, set the agenda for debates about meaning and metaphysics. Carnap's work on semantics and probability remains influential. They demonstrated philosophy could be rigorous without being speculative.

**Future Exploration:**
- Is any version of the verification principle defensible?
- What survives of logical positivism?
- How did Carnap's views evolve from *Aufbau* to later work?
- Was emotivism refuted or refined (expressivism)?

---

#### W.V.O. Quine (1908 – 2000)

**Core Problems:** Epistemology, philosophy of language, philosophy of logic, metaphysics

**Central Ideas:**
- **"Two Dogmas of Empiricism":** Attack on positivism's foundations
  - First dogma: analytic-synthetic distinction is untenable
  - No principled way to distinguish truth-by-meaning from truth-by-fact
  - Analyticity, synonymy, meaning—all in same circle
  - Second dogma: reductionism (statements have individual empirical content)
  - Both dogmas are "ill-founded"
- **Confirmation holism:** Statements face experience as a corporate body
  - No statement is immune to revision (even logic, math)
  - No statement is confirmed or disconfirmed in isolation
  - "The unit of empirical significance is the whole of science"
  - Any recalcitrant experience can be accommodated by adjusting elsewhere
  - Web of belief, not foundation and superstructure
- **Naturalized epistemology:** Epistemology as part of psychology
  - Reject first philosophy—no standpoint outside science
  - Study how we actually form beliefs, not how we should ideally
  - Epistemology continuous with cognitive science
  - "Epistemology... falls into place as a chapter of psychology"
- **Indeterminacy of translation:** No fact of the matter about meaning
  - Radical translation scenario: translating unknown language from behavior
  - Multiple incompatible translation manuals can fit all behavior
  - No fact of the matter which is "correct"
  - Meaning is not a determinate property
- **Ontological relativity:** What exists is relative to a background theory
  - Questions of existence only make sense within a theory
  - "To be is to be the value of a variable"
  - Inscrutability of reference: words don't determinately refer

**Key Works:**
- "Two Dogmas of Empiricism" (1951) — critique of positivism
- *Word and Object* (1960) — indeterminacy, ontological relativity
- "Epistemology Naturalized" (1969) — naturalized epistemology
- *Ontological Relativity and Other Essays* (1969)
- *From a Logical Point of View* (1953) — essays including "Two Dogmas"

**Connections:**
- *Influenced by*: Carnap (his teacher, then critic), positivism, Duhem
- *Influences*: Davidson, naturalism in philosophy, philosophy of language
- *Criticized*: Analytic-synthetic distinction, meaning, mentalistic semantics
- *Debates with*: Carnap, Chomsky (on language acquisition)

**Common Misconceptions:**
- Quine wasn't an irrationalist—he was a thoroughgoing naturalist
- Holism doesn't mean "anything goes"—some revisions are more plausible
- Indeterminacy isn't skepticism—it's about what facts there are
- Naturalized epistemology isn't eliminative—it's still epistemology
- He remained an empiricist—just a reformed, holistic one

**Why He Matters:** "Two Dogmas" may be the most important paper in 20th-century analytic philosophy, dismantling logical positivism from within. Quine's holism transformed epistemology and philosophy of science. Naturalized epistemology reconnected philosophy to cognitive science. His influence on Davidson and subsequent philosophy of language is profound.

**Future Exploration:**
- Is the analytic-synthetic distinction really untenable?
- Does holism lead to relativism?
- Can naturalized epistemology be normative?
- What are the implications of indeterminacy of translation?
- How does Quine's view differ from Kuhn's on theory change?

---

#### Ordinary Language Philosophy: J.L. Austin (1911 – 1960) & Gilbert Ryle (1900 – 1976)

**Core Problems:** Philosophy of language, philosophy of mind, philosophical methodology

**Central Ideas (Austin):**
- **Speech act theory:** Language does things, not just says things
  - Performative utterances: "I promise," "I name this ship"—not true/false, but felicitous/infelicitous
  - Constative utterances: ordinary statements that are true or false
  - But the distinction breaks down—all utterances have performative aspects
- **Locutionary, illocutionary, perlocutionary acts:** Three dimensions of speech
  - Locutionary: saying something (meaning, reference)
  - Illocutionary: what we do *in* saying (promise, warn, assert)
  - Perlocutionary: what we achieve *by* saying (persuade, frighten, amuse)
  - Illocutionary force central to communication
- **Felicity conditions:** When speech acts succeed or fail
  - Conventions must be followed (can't baptize a penguin casually)
  - Sincerity required (insincere promise still a promise, but defective)
  - Various ways speech acts can "misfire"

**Central Ideas (Ryle):**
- **The ghost in the machine:** Critique of Cartesian dualism
  - Mind isn't a separate substance inside the body
  - This picture commits a category mistake
  - Like asking "Where is the university?" after seeing all the buildings
- **Category mistakes:** Philosophical confusions from misplaced concepts
  - Treating "mind" as the name of a special thing
  - Confusing different logical types of expressions
  - Many philosophical problems dissolve when mistake is seen
- **Knowing how vs. knowing that:** Two kinds of knowledge
  - Knowing how (skills) is not reducible to knowing that (propositions)
  - Intelligence shown in performance, not prior to it
  - Regress argument: applying rules requires knowing how to apply them
- **Dispositions:** Mental terms refer to behavioral dispositions
  - "Believes," "wants," "knows"—not names of inner states
  - They describe patterns of behavior and behavioral potential
  - Not strict behaviorism—dispositions are more than actual behavior

**Key Works:**
- Austin: *How to Do Things with Words* (1962), "A Plea for Excuses" (1956)
- Ryle: *The Concept of Mind* (1949), "Systematically Misleading Expressions" (1932)

**Connections:**
- *Oxford philosophy*: Moore's influence, attention to ordinary language
- *Austin influenced*: Searle (speech acts), pragmatics, philosophy of law
- *Ryle influenced*: Philosophy of mind, action theory, skill epistemology
- *Opposed to*: Cartesianism, traditional metaphysics, artificial technical vocabularies

**Common Misconceptions:**
- Ordinary language philosophy isn't about what "the man on the street" says
- It's not anti-theoretical—it uses ordinary language to dissolve pseudo-problems
- Ryle's "behaviorism" isn't Watson's—it's about the logic of mental concepts
- Austin wasn't just classifying—speech act theory has substantive implications
- They weren't relativists about ordinary language—they thought careful attention reveals truth

**Why They Matter:** Austin founded speech act theory, transforming philosophy of language and influencing linguistics, law, and literary theory. Ryle's critique of Cartesianism anticipated later philosophy of mind. Their methods—careful attention to actual usage—defined Oxford philosophy for a generation. The knowing-how/knowing-that distinction remains central to epistemology.

**Future Exploration:**
- Is knowing how reducible to knowing that?
- Does speech act theory succeed as a general theory of meaning?
- Is the category mistake diagnosis of philosophical problems too easy?
- What's the relationship between ordinary language philosophy and Wittgenstein?

---

#### John Rawls (1921 – 2002)

**Core Problems:** Political philosophy, ethics (distributive justice), moral methodology

**Central Ideas:**
- **Justice as fairness:** Principles chosen from a fair standpoint
  - Just principles are those rational persons would agree to under fair conditions
  - Social contract tradition, but hypothetical rather than historical
  - Procedure is what makes outcome just
  - Alternative to utilitarianism in political philosophy
- **The original position:** Thought experiment for choosing principles
  - Hypothetical choosers deciding basic structure of society
  - Not real people—"artificial persons" representing citizens
  - Device for clarifying our considered judgments about justice
  - What would rational persons choose?
- **The veil of ignorance:** Ensures impartiality
  - Choosers don't know their race, sex, class, talents, conception of good
  - Forces them to consider everyone's perspective
  - Can't rig principles in own favor
  - Impartiality follows from ignorance of particulars
- **Two principles of justice:** Result of the original position
  - *First principle* (liberty): Equal basic liberties for all
  - *Second principle* (difference): Inequalities only if they benefit the least advantaged
  - First principle has priority—can't trade liberty for wealth
  - Difference principle: not equality, but justifiable inequality
- **Reflective equilibrium:** Method of moral justification
  - Move back and forth between principles and considered judgments
  - Adjust either until they cohere
  - No foundational starting point—coherence is justification
  - Influential methodology beyond Rawls's specific theory
- **Political liberalism:** Justice for pluralistic societies
  - Later work: how can people with different comprehensive doctrines agree?
  - Public reason: political arguments should be accessible to all
  - Overlapping consensus: different reasons, same conclusions
  - Justice is political, not metaphysical

**Key Works:**
- *A Theory of Justice* (1971) — systematic political philosophy
- *Political Liberalism* (1993) — justice amid pluralism
- *Justice as Fairness: A Restatement* (2001) — final statement
- *The Law of Peoples* (1999) — international justice

**Connections:**
- *Influenced by*: Kant (proceduralism, respect for persons), social contract tradition
- *Influences*: All subsequent political philosophy, applied ethics
- *Critics*: Nozick (libertarian), communitarians (Sandel, MacIntyre, Walzer), feminists, Marxists
- *Debate with*: Utilitarians—Rawls's theory is explicitly anti-utilitarian

**Common Misconceptions:**
- The original position isn't a historical event—it's a thought experiment
- Rawls isn't egalitarian—he permits inequality if it benefits worst-off
- The veil isn't total ignorance—choosers know general facts about society
- Political liberalism isn't relativism—it's about justification to reasonable people
- Rawls wasn't naive—he engaged extensively with critics

**Why He Matters:** *A Theory of Justice* revived political philosophy after decades of quietism. Rawls provided a systematic alternative to utilitarianism and showed how liberal principles could be justified. Reflective equilibrium became standard methodology. Even critics define their positions against his. Probably the most influential political philosopher of the 20th century.

**Future Exploration:**
- Does the original position really yield the two principles?
- Can the veil of ignorance be coherently described?
- How does political liberalism relate to the original theory?
- Does the difference principle justify enough redistribution?
- Can Rawlsian justice be extended globally?

---

#### Saul Kripke (1940 – 2022)

**Core Problems:** Philosophy of language, metaphysics, philosophy of mind, philosophy of logic, philosophy of mathematics

**Central Ideas:**
- **Rigid designators:** Names refer to the same individual in all possible worlds
  - Proper names like "Aristotle" pick out the same person across counterfactual scenarios
  - Contrasts with descriptions, which pick out whoever satisfies them
  - "The teacher of Alexander" might pick out different people in different possible worlds
  - But "Aristotle" rigidly designates Aristotle—even in worlds where he never taught anyone
  - Revolutionary break from Frege-Russell descriptivist theory of names
- **The causal-historical theory of reference:** Names refer through causal chains, not descriptions
  - An initial "baptism" connects a name to its bearer
  - The name passes through a community of speakers preserving reference
  - We can use names successfully without knowing identifying descriptions
  - Explains how we can be wrong about everything we believe about a referent
  - Reference is a social, historical phenomenon, not a matter of individual mental content
- **Necessary a posteriori truths:** Some truths are both necessary and knowable only through experience
  - "Hesperus is Phosphorus" (both names rigidly designate Venus)
  - If true at all, it's necessarily true—the same object can't fail to be itself
  - But we discover this through empirical astronomy, not pure reason
  - "Water is H₂O"—necessarily true but empirically discovered
  - Shatters the traditional equation of necessary with a priori, contingent with a posteriori
- **Possible worlds semantics:** Possible worlds as a framework for analyzing modal notions
  - Necessity: true in all possible worlds
  - Possibility: true in at least one possible world
  - Kripke formalized this into rigorous modal logic as a teenager
  - But worlds are stipulated, not discovered—"possible worlds are stipulated, not discovered"
  - Distinguished from Lewis's modal realism—Kripke treats worlds as abstract stipulations
- **Anti-materialism argument from modal considerations:** Against mind-brain identity
  - If pain = C-fiber firing, this identity must be necessary (like heat = molecular motion)
  - But we can conceive of pain without C-fibers (and vice versa)
  - Unlike water/H₂O, the appearance of contingency isn't explained away
  - Pain is picked out by its felt quality, not by a contingent mode of presentation
  - Therefore, pain ≠ C-fiber firing; physicalism about the mental faces a serious challenge
- **Rule-following and meaning skepticism (Wittgenstein interpretation):** The problem of what determines meaning
  - *Wittgenstein on Rules and Private Language* advances "Kripkenstein" argument
  - No fact about past usage determines what I mean by "plus"
  - Maybe I mean "quus" (like plus, but for numbers under 57, yields 5 for larger)
  - All my past calculations are consistent with either interpretation
  - Skeptical solution: meaning is constituted by community agreement, not private facts
  - Hugely influential reading of Wittgenstein, whether or not it captures Wittgenstein's intent

**Key Works:**
- *Naming and Necessity* (1980, from 1970 lectures) — revolutionized philosophy of language and metaphysics
- *Wittgenstein on Rules and Private Language* (1982) — the rule-following problem and meaning skepticism
- "A Completeness Theorem in Modal Logic" (1959) — seminal contribution to modal logic at age 18
- "Identity and Necessity" (1971) — central arguments about identity and modality
- "Outline of a Theory of Truth" (1975) — approach to the liar paradox
- *Reference and Existence* (2013) — on fictional and empty names

**Connections:**
- *Influenced by*: Frege, Russell (as targets), Wittgenstein, Quine (as target), Mill (on names), Leibniz
- *Engages with*: Putnam (collaborator on semantic externalism), Donnellan, Marcus
- *Influences*: Contemporary philosophy of language, metaphysics revival, Williamson, Soames, semantic externalism

**Common Misconceptions:**
- Kripke doesn't think possible worlds are concrete universes (that's Lewis)—they're abstract stipulations
- The causal theory doesn't require direct causal contact—it works through social chains
- Kripkenstein's skeptical paradox may not be Kripke's own view—it's his reading of Wittgenstein
- Kripke's anti-physicalism argument isn't a proof—it's a challenge physicalists must answer

**Why He Matters:** *Naming and Necessity* is one of the most influential philosophy books of the late 20th century. Kripke overturned the Frege-Russell orthodoxy on names, revived essentialist metaphysics (properties can be necessary without being a priori), provided new tools for philosophy of mind debates, and transformed modal logic from a technical curiosity into a central philosophical tool. His work embodies analytic philosophy's ideal: formal precision addressing traditional questions about language, knowledge, and reality.

**Future Exploration:**
- How does Kripke's essentialism differ from traditional Aristotelian essentialism?
- Can the causal theory handle reference-borrowing and reference-change?
- What's the relationship between Kripkenstein and Wittgenstein's actual view?
- How does the modal argument against materialism interact with contemporary physicalism?
- What makes possible worlds genuine possibilities rather than arbitrary stipulations?

---

#### David Lewis (1941 – 2001)

**Core Problems:** Metaphysics, philosophy of language, philosophy of mind, epistemology, philosophy of science, ethics

**Central Ideas:**
- **Modal realism:** Possible worlds are real, concrete entities—not abstract or fictional
  - Every way the world could have been is a way some world is
  - Other possible worlds exist just as our world does—they're simply spatiotemporally isolated
  - "Actual" is indexical, like "here"—each world is actual relative to its inhabitants
  - Ontologically extravagant but theoretically powerful and unified
  - Provides reductive analyses of modality, properties, propositions, and more
- **Counterpart theory:** Transworld identity replaced by similarity relations
  - Individuals don't exist in multiple possible worlds
  - Your "counterpart" in another world is someone sufficiently similar to you
  - "You might have been a lawyer" = there's a world where your counterpart is a lawyer
  - Solves problems about identity across worlds
  - Similarity is context-dependent—different counterparts for different questions
- **Humean supervenience:** All facts supervene on the distribution of local qualities
  - "A vast mosaic of local matters of particular fact"
  - The world is just one thing after another in spacetime
  - Laws, causation, chances, dispositions—all supervene on this mosaic
  - No primitive necessary connections between distinct existences
  - Defense of Hume's metaphysics with modern logical tools
- **Best systems analysis of laws:** Laws as axioms of the best systematization of facts
  - Laws are regularities that appear as axioms in the best theory of the world
  - "Best" = optimal balance of simplicity and strength (informativeness)
  - No primitive nomological necessity—laws are descriptive, not prescriptive
  - Explains why laws seem to "govern" without mysterious modal force
  - Chances as features of the best system (Humean objective chance)
- **Counterfactual analysis of causation:** Causation as counterfactual dependence
  - C causes E iff if C hadn't occurred, E wouldn't have occurred
  - Analyzed via similarity of possible worlds
  - Requires careful handling of preemption, overdetermination, transitivity
  - Later modified to influence theory: causes alter probability, timing, manner of effects
  - Foundation for extensive literature on causation
- **Convention:** Coordination problems and the nature of social regularities
  - Conventions are solutions to coordination problems
  - Language is a convention—regularities in a community solving communication problems
  - No need for explicit agreements; conventions emerge from mutual expectations
  - Rational self-interest and precedent sustain them
  - Influenced social philosophy and philosophy of language
- **Philosophy of mind:** Functionalism, folk psychology, phenomenal character
  - Mental states defined by their causal/functional roles
  - "Mad pain and Martian pain"—functionalism must accommodate both
  - Qualia realism: there are genuine phenomenal properties
  - The knowledge argument (Mary's room): Lewis's "ability hypothesis" response—Mary gains know-how, not new facts

**Key Works:**
- *Counterfactuals* (1973) — rigorous semantics for counterfactual conditionals
- *Convention* (1969) — landmark analysis of conventions as coordination solutions
- *On the Plurality of Worlds* (1986) — comprehensive defense of modal realism
- *Parts of Classes* (1991) — on set theory and mereology
- *Philosophical Papers* (2 volumes, 1983-1986) — collected essays covering vast range
- "Causation" (1973) and "Causation as Influence" (2000) — counterfactual theory and refinements
- "Mad Pain and Martian Pain" (1980) — on functionalism and phenomenal qualities
- "Elusive Knowledge" (1996) — contextualist epistemology
- "Humean Supervenience Debugged" (1994) — defending Humeanism about chance

**Connections:**
- *Influenced by*: Hume, Quine, Carnap, Stalnaker (possible worlds), Kripke (modal logic)
- *Engages with*: Armstrong (on laws), van Fraassen, Putnam, Jackson
- *Influences*: Contemporary metaphysics, Schaffer, Sider, Hawthorne, philosophy of physics, philosophy of biology

**Common Misconceptions:**
- Modal realism isn't eliminativism about modality—Lewis believes in genuine possibility and necessity
- Lewis didn't think we could travel to other worlds—they're spatiotemporally isolated by definition
- "Actual" being indexical doesn't mean nothing is really actual—our world is actual (relative to us)
- Lewis was a realist, not an instrumentalist—other worlds really exist

**Why He Matters:** Lewis was perhaps the most systematic metaphysician of the late 20th century. His defense of modal realism, even if few accept it, set the agenda for modal metaphysics. His analyses of causation, laws, counterfactuals, and convention remain foundational. His style—creative thought experiments, rigorous argument, willingness to bite bullets—exemplifies contemporary analytic philosophy. Across every topic he touched, Lewis raised the bar for clarity and depth.

**Future Exploration:**
- Is modal realism's parsimony advantage real, given its massive ontology?
- How does Humean supervenience fare in light of quantum mechanics?
- Can counterpart theory capture our modal judgments about ourselves?
- What determines which system counts as "best" for the best systems account?
- How does Lewis's framework apply to ethical modalities and evaluative discourse?

---

#### Derek Parfit (1942 – 2017)

**Core Problems:** Personal identity, ethics (normative and meta), rationality, population ethics, meaning of life

**Central Ideas:**
- **Reductionist view of personal identity:** The self reduces to more basic facts
  - There's no "deep further fact" about personal identity beyond psychological and physical continuity
  - What matters isn't identity per se but psychological continuity and connectedness
  - "Relation R"—psychological connectedness (memories, intentions, beliefs, desires) and continuity
  - We are not separately existing entities, as Cartesian egos
  - Buddhist-influenced view: the self is more like a process than a thing
- **Personal identity doesn't matter:** What we care about isn't identity as such
  - Even if identity is indeterminate, what matters (Relation R) may not be
  - Branching cases: if you split into two people, neither is you—but both have what matters
  - Survival can come in degrees; identity cannot
  - This should reduce our self-interested concern and excessive individualism
  - Liberation: realizing the truth about identity should change how we live
- **Teleportation and fission cases:** Thought experiments revealing our intuitions
  - Teletransportation: body destroyed, exact replica created—do you survive?
  - Fission: brain split and each half creates a full person—which (if either) is you?
  - These cases show identity questions can lack determinate answers
  - What we should care about (psychological continuity) can diverge from identity
  - Practical importance: how we think about future selves, death, and ethics
- **The Non-Identity Problem:** Future people's existence depends on our choices
  - Different policies create different future people (at different times, circumstances)
  - If we choose a worse policy, the people who exist aren't harmed—they wouldn't exist otherwise
  - But surely some choices are worse for future generations
  - How can we harm someone by bringing them into existence?
  - Challenges person-affecting principles in ethics
- **The Mere Addition Paradox (Repugnant Conclusion):** Challenge to total utilitarianism
  - Adding people with positive but low welfare can't make things worse
  - But repeated addition leads to vast populations with barely worth-living lives
  - This seems better than a smaller population with excellent lives (total utilitarianism)
  - But that conclusion is repugnant—surely quality matters, not just total welfare
  - Population ethics may have no satisfactory theory
- **Triple Theory (convergence of ethical traditions):** Major ethical theories partially converge
  - Kantianism, consequentialism, and contractualism, properly understood, often agree
  - Rule consequentialism and Kantian principles yield similar conclusions
  - Parfit sought to show that ethics is more unified than sectarian disputes suggest
  - If we could agree on concrete moral questions, disagreement about foundations matters less
  - Normative ethics can be like mathematics—objectively true
- **Reasons fundamentalism:** Normative reasons as irreducibly fundamental
  - Reasons for action and belief aren't reducible to desires or other natural facts
  - Against Humean theory of motivation—we can have reason-based motivation
  - Normativity is a basic feature of reality
  - Cognitivist and non-naturalist: moral claims are true or false, and not natural facts

**Key Works:**
- *Reasons and Persons* (1984) — masterpiece on personal identity, rationality, and population ethics
- *On What Matters* (Vol. 1-2: 2011; Vol. 3: 2017) — comprehensive work on normative ethics
- "Personal Identity" (1971) — influential early paper on reductionism
- "Later Selves and Moral Principles" (1973) — implications of identity views for ethics
- "Future Generations: Further Problems" (1976) — the Non-Identity Problem

**Connections:**
- *Influenced by*: Hume (on self), Sidgwick (ethics), Williams (discussions of identity), Buddhist philosophy, Locke (memory theory)
- *Engages with*: Nozick, Nagel, Scanlon, Thomson, Korsgaard, Singer
- *Influences*: Contemporary ethics, effective altruism movement, population ethics, MacAskill, philosophy of future generations

**Common Misconceptions:**
- Parfit didn't think personal identity is "unreal"—he thought it's real but not what matters
- Reductionism isn't nihilism about persons—persons exist, just not as fundamental entities
- Parfit wasn't indifferent to individual welfare—his ethical work was motivated by concern for wellbeing
- The Repugnant Conclusion isn't Parfit's view—it's a problem he highlighted for utilitarian theories

**Why He Matters:** *Reasons and Persons* is one of the most important philosophy books of the 20th century, transforming discussions of personal identity and introducing problems (Non-Identity, Repugnant Conclusion) that remain central. Parfit showed how abstract metaphysics connects to how we should live. His later work sought to reconcile ethical traditions that had seemed incompatible. He combined philosophical rigor with moral seriousness, and his work on future generations has influenced the effective altruism movement and debates about existential risk.

**Future Exploration:**
- How should Parfit's view of personal identity affect practical decisions about death, punishment, and responsibility?
- Can any population ethics avoid all the paradoxes Parfit identified?
- Does the Triple Theory show genuine convergence or merely similar conclusions from different foundations?
- How does Parfit's reasons fundamentalism compare to naturalist alternatives?
- What's the relationship between Parfit's reductionism and Buddhist conceptions of the self?

### Continental Tradition

> *Existence, meaning, and the structures of experience*

#### Edmund Husserl (1859 – 1938)

**Core Problems:** Epistemology, philosophy of mind, logic, ontology, foundations of science

**Central Ideas:**
- **Phenomenology as rigorous science:** Philosophy should be presuppositionless description of experience
  - "To the things themselves!" (Zu den Sachen selbst!)
  - Rigorous method, not speculation or metaphysical system-building
  - Foundational science grounding all other sciences
- **Intentionality:** Consciousness is always consciousness *of* something
  - Borrowed from Brentano, transformed into central concept
  - Mental acts are directed toward objects (real or imagined)
  - **Noesis:** The act of consciousness (perceiving, judging, remembering)
  - **Noema:** The object or content as intended
  - Intentionality is the essential structure of consciousness
- **Epoché and phenomenological reduction:** Methodological suspension of the "natural attitude"
  - **Natural attitude:** Our everyday assumption that the world exists independently
  - **Epoché (bracketing):** Suspend judgment about existence; focus on how things appear
  - **Transcendental reduction:** Turn attention to the constituting acts of consciousness
  - Not denying the world exists; setting aside that question to study experience as such
- **Transcendental consciousness:** The sphere revealed by the reduction
  - Pure consciousness as the field of phenomenological investigation
  - How objects are "constituted" in and through conscious experience
  - The ego as the pole of all intentional acts
- **Eidetic intuition:** Grasping essential structures through imaginative variation
  - What is invariant across all possible variations of a phenomenon?
  - Yields necessary truths about types of experience
  - Not empirical generalization but intuition of essences (eide)
- **The lifeworld (Lebenswelt):** The pre-scientific world of everyday experience
  - Science abstracts from the lifeworld; phenomenology returns to it
  - Critique of scientific naturalism for forgetting its experiential foundations
  - Later work emphasized intersubjectivity and the social world
- **Time-consciousness:** Fundamental structure of inner experience
  - Retention (just-past), primal impression (now), protention (anticipation)
  - Time not a container but constituted in the flow of experience

**Key Works:**
- *Logical Investigations* (1900–1901) — founding text of phenomenology
- *Ideas Pertaining to a Pure Phenomenology* I (1913) — transcendental phenomenology
- *Cartesian Meditations* (1931) — summary of his philosophy
- *The Crisis of European Sciences* (1936) — lifeworld, critique of scientific naturalism
- *Ideas* II and III (posthumous) — constitution of nature and the body

**Connections:**
- *Influenced by*: Brentano (intentionality), Frege (logic), Descartes (methodological doubt)
- *Influences*: Heidegger (student), Sartre, Merleau-Ponty, Levinas, Derrida, all of phenomenology
- *Contrasts with*: Naturalism, psychologism, naïve realism

**Common Misconceptions:**
- The epoché isn't doubt about the world's existence—it's methodological suspension of that question
- Husserl isn't a subjective idealist denying external reality
- Phenomenology isn't introspective psychology—it studies essential structures, not individual mental events
- The later Husserl emphasized intersubjectivity; he wasn't solipsistic

**Why He Matters:** Founded phenomenology, one of the 20th century's major philosophical movements. His method influenced Heidegger, Sartre, Merleau-Ponty, and much of continental philosophy. The concept of intentionality remains central to philosophy of mind. His critique of naturalism and recovery of the lifeworld anticipated later debates about science and meaning.

**Future Exploration:**
- Does the epoché really achieve presuppositionlessness?
- Can transcendental phenomenology escape solipsism?
- What is the relationship between Husserl's and Brentano's intentionality?
- How does phenomenology relate to cognitive science?
- Is phenomenology a science or a philosophy?

---

#### Martin Heidegger (1889 – 1976)

**Core Problems:** Ontology (the meaning of Being), philosophy of mind, philosophy of language, philosophy of technology

**Central Ideas:**
- **The question of Being (Seinsfrage):** What does it mean for something *to be*?
  - Philosophy has forgotten this question since the Greeks
  - We operate with vague understanding of Being; need to make it explicit
  - Not asking "what beings exist?" but "what is Being as such?"
- **Dasein:** The being that asks about Being—human existence
  - German for "being-there" or "existence"
  - We are not subjects confronting objects; we are always already in a world
  - Dasein is characterized by understanding of Being, even if pre-reflective
  - Access to Being is through analysis of Dasein's structures
- **Being-in-the-world:** Dasein's fundamental way of existing
  - We don't first exist, then enter a world; we are constitutively worldly
  - The world is not a container but a web of meaningful relations
  - Equipment (Zuhandenheit): things show up as "ready-to-hand" for purposes
  - Breakdown reveals things as merely "present-at-hand" (Vorhandenheit)
- **Thrownness (Geworfenheit):** We find ourselves already in a situation we didn't choose
  - Born into a particular time, culture, language, body
  - Facticity: we have a past that shapes but doesn't determine us
  - We are "thrown projection"—shaped by past but projecting toward future
- **Being-toward-death:** Death is Dasein's ownmost possibility
  - Not an event at the end of life but a structure of existence
  - My death is mine alone; it individualizes
  - Authentically facing death reveals what truly matters
  - Flight from death into the "they" (das Man) is inauthentic
- **Authenticity (Eigentlichkeit) and inauthenticity:** Ways of existing
  - Inauthenticity: absorbed in the "they," doing what "one" does, conforming
  - Authenticity: owning one's existence, resolute choice, being oneself
  - Not a moral judgment; inauthenticity is Dasein's default mode
  - **Anxiety (Angst):** Reveals the nothing, individualizes, enables authenticity
- **Temporality:** The meaning of Dasein's being is time
  - Not clock time but lived temporality
  - Ecstatic unity of past (having-been), present (making-present), future (ahead-of-itself)
  - Authentic temporality: anticipatory resoluteness
- **Later philosophy ("the turn"):** Shift from Dasein to Being itself
  - History of Being: different epochs reveal Being differently
  - Technology: modern age sees everything as "standing reserve" for use
  - Poetry and language: "language is the house of Being"
  - Dwelling: authentic relation to earth, sky, mortals, divinities

**Key Works:**
- *Being and Time* (1927) — incomplete magnum opus
- "What Is Metaphysics?" (1929)
- "The Origin of the Work of Art" (1935)
- *Contributions to Philosophy (From Enowning)* (1936–38, published 1989)
- "Letter on Humanism" (1946)
- "The Question Concerning Technology" (1954)
- "Building Dwelling Thinking" (1951)

**Connections:**
- *Influenced by*: Husserl (phenomenology), Aristotle, pre-Socratics, Kierkegaard, Nietzsche
- *Influences*: Sartre, Gadamer, Arendt, Derrida, Foucault, postmodernism, hermeneutics
- *Criticized by*: Carnap, analytic philosophers, critics of his Nazism

**Controversy:** Heidegger joined the Nazi Party in 1933 and served as rector of Freiburg University. He never clearly repudiated Nazism. The Black Notebooks (published 2014) revealed antisemitic remarks. The relationship between his philosophy and politics remains debated. Some see deep connections; others distinguish his thought from his politics.

**Common Misconceptions:**
- Heidegger isn't simply an existentialist—he rejected that label
- "Dasein" isn't equivalent to "consciousness" or "the subject"
- Authenticity isn't individualism or selfishness
- His difficulty isn't obscurantism—he's trying to express what ordinary language conceals

**Why He Matters:** Most influential continental philosopher of the 20th century. *Being and Time* transformed phenomenology and shaped existentialism, hermeneutics, and deconstruction. His analysis of technology, dwelling, and the forgetfulness of Being remains urgent. His influence extends beyond philosophy to architecture, ecology, theology, and literary criticism.

**Future Exploration:**
- What exactly is the question of Being, and can it be answered?
- Is the relationship between Heidegger's philosophy and Nazism essential or accidental?
- Does *Being and Time* need the unpublished sections to be complete?
- What is the relationship between early and later Heidegger?
- Is Heidegger's critique of technology reactionary or prophetic?

---

#### Jean-Paul Sartre (1905 – 1980)

**Core Problems:** Ontology, philosophy of mind, ethics, political philosophy, philosophy of literature

**Central Ideas:**
- **Existence precedes essence:** We are not born with a fixed nature
  - Humans first exist, then define themselves through choices
  - There is no human nature determining what we must be
  - We are "condemned to be free"—we must choose without guidance
  - Contrast with objects (in-itself) whose essence precedes existence
- **Being-in-itself (en-soi) vs. being-for-itself (pour-soi):**
  - **In-itself:** Things, objects—complete, solid, identical with themselves
  - **For-itself:** Consciousness—characterized by lack, negation, transcendence
  - Consciousness is "what it is not, and is not what it is"
  - The for-itself desires the impossible: to be both free and complete (God)
- **Radical freedom:** We are absolutely free in our consciousness
  - No situation determines our response; we choose our attitude
  - Facticity (given conditions) is always surpassed by transcendence (projects)
  - We are responsible for everything we make of ourselves
  - "Man is nothing other than what he makes of himself"
- **Anguish (angoisse):** The dizziness of freedom
  - Awareness of our freedom and total responsibility
  - Nothing guarantees our choices; no excuses are valid
  - Anguish before the openness of the future
- **Bad faith (mauvaise foi):** Self-deception about our freedom
  - Fleeing from freedom by pretending to be determined
  - The waiter who plays at being a waiter—denying he could choose otherwise
  - Also: denying facticity by pretending to be pure transcendence
  - Bad faith is lying to oneself about the nature of consciousness
- **The look (le regard):** Being-for-others
  - The Other's gaze objectifies me, makes me an object in their world
  - Shame reveals I am what the Other sees me as
  - Conflict is the original meaning of being-for-others
  - "Hell is other people"—we are trapped in others' judgments
- **Nothingness and negation:** Consciousness brings nothingness into the world
  - We can negate what is (this is not that)
  - We imagine what is not (projects, possibilities)
  - Freedom is the power of negation
- **Commitment (engagement):** Freedom implies responsibility to act
  - Choosing for myself, I choose for all humanity
  - Political engagement is demanded; neutrality is bad faith
  - Later: turn toward Marxism and collective action
  - The writer must be "engaged"—committed to social justice

**Key Works:**
- *The Transcendence of the Ego* (1936)
- *Nausea* (1938) — novel
- *Being and Nothingness* (1943) — main philosophical work
- *No Exit* (1944) — play
- *Existentialism Is a Humanism* (1946) — popular lecture
- *Critique of Dialectical Reason* (1960) — Marxist turn
- *The Words* (1964) — autobiography

**Connections:**
- *Influenced by*: Husserl (phenomenology), Heidegger (ontology), Descartes, Hegel
- *Dialogue with*: Merleau-Ponty (friend, later rival), Camus (friend, later broke), Beauvoir (partner)
- *Influences*: Existentialism broadly, postcolonial thought (Fanon), radical politics
- *Criticized by*: Heidegger (rejected Sartre's reading), structuralists, Marxists

**Common Misconceptions:**
- Existentialism isn't nihilism or despair—it's about taking responsibility
- "Existence precedes essence" doesn't mean anything goes—we are responsible
- "Hell is other people" doesn't mean we should avoid others; it's about objectification
- Sartre didn't ignore social conditions; his later work emphasized them
- He refused the Nobel Prize (1964) on principle

**Why He Matters:** Made existentialism a global movement. *Being and Nothingness* is a landmark of 20th-century philosophy. United philosophical rigor with literary expression. Showed philosophy could engage with politics, literature, and lived experience. His concepts—bad faith, radical freedom, the look—became part of intellectual culture.

**Future Exploration:**
- Is radical freedom compatible with acknowledging social constraints?
- Can we escape bad faith, or are we always in some form of self-deception?
- What is the relationship between early (existentialist) and late (Marxist) Sartre?
- How does Sartre's view of others compare to Levinas's ethics of the Other?
- Is existentialism a humanism, or does it undermine humanism?

---

#### Simone de Beauvoir (1908 – 1986)

**Core Problems:** Ethics (ambiguity, freedom), feminism (women's oppression), existentialism, political philosophy

**Central Ideas:**
- **"One is not born, but rather becomes, a woman":** Gender is constructed
  - Foundational claim of feminist philosophy
  - Sex (biological) vs. gender (social construction) distinction
  - Femininity is a situation imposed on women, not an essence
  - Women are made, through upbringing and society, not nature
  - Anticipates later feminist and gender theory
- **Woman as "the Other":** Patriarchy defines woman as not-man
  - "Humanity is male, and man defines woman not in herself, but as relative to him"
  - Man is the Subject, the Absolute; woman is the Other
  - Women's subjectivity is denied; they are defined by male perspective
  - Applies Hegel's master-slave dialectic to gender
- **The ethics of ambiguity:** Existentialist ethics
  - Existence is fundamentally ambiguous—we are both free and situated
  - Against the "spirit of seriousness": treating abstractions as fixed values
  - Freedom entails responsibility; our projects affect others
  - Genuine freedom requires willing the freedom of others
  - Ethics is about creating meaning through action
- **Immanence and transcendence:** Two modes of existence
  - Transcendence: projecting oneself toward future possibilities
  - Immanence: being confined to repetitive, passive existence
  - Women historically confined to immanence (domestic sphere)
  - Liberation requires access to transcendence—work, politics, creativity
- **Situated freedom:** Freedom within constraints
  - We are always in a situation—embodied, historical, social
  - Freedom isn't abstract; it must be exercised in concrete conditions
  - Oppression can limit but not eliminate freedom
  - Critiques Sartre's more abstract conception of freedom

**Key Works:**
- *The Second Sex* (1949) — foundational feminist philosophy
- *The Ethics of Ambiguity* (1947) — existentialist ethics
- *She Came to Stay* (1943) — philosophical novel on consciousness and others
- *The Mandarins* (1954) — novel on postwar intellectuals (Prix Goncourt)
- Memoirs (4 volumes) — philosophical autobiography

**Connections:**
- *With*: Sartre (lifelong partner, philosophical collaborator)
- *Influenced by*: Hegel, Husserl, Heidegger, Marx
- *Influences*: Second-wave feminism, feminist philosophy, gender studies
- *Contemporary with*: Merleau-Ponty, Camus

**Common Misconceptions:**
- She wasn't simply "Sartre's companion"—she was a major philosopher in her own right
- *The Second Sex* isn't just sociology—it's rigorous philosophical analysis
- She modified Sartrean existentialism significantly (situated freedom)
- The ethics of ambiguity isn't relativism—it has normative commitments
- Her feminism was radical for its time, even if superseded in some respects

**Why She Matters:** *The Second Sex* is the founding text of modern feminist philosophy and one of the most influential books of the 20th century. Beauvoir showed how existentialist concepts—freedom, situation, the Other—illuminate women's oppression. Her ethics of ambiguity provides a framework for existentialist ethics that Sartre never fully developed.

**Future Exploration:**
- How does Beauvoir's feminism relate to later waves?
- Is the sex/gender distinction sustainable?
- How does her ethics compare to Sartre's?
- What's the relationship between freedom and situation?
- How should we understand "becoming" a woman today?

---

#### Maurice Merleau-Ponty (1908 – 1961)

**Core Problems:** Phenomenology (perception, embodiment), philosophy of mind, ontology, philosophy of language

**Central Ideas:**
- **The primacy of perception:** Perception is fundamental
  - Philosophy must begin with lived perceptual experience
  - Perception is not passive reception but active bodily engagement
  - The perceived world is the foundation for all knowledge
  - Against both empiricism (sensations) and intellectualism (ideas)
- **The body-subject:** We are our bodies
  - The body is not an object I have but a subject I am
  - "It is through my body that I understand other people"
  - Bodily intentionality: the body "understands" before conscious thought
  - Habit, gesture, and skill are forms of bodily knowledge
  - Against Cartesian dualism: body and mind are unified
- **Being-in-the-world:** Our fundamental mode of existence
  - We are always already embedded in a meaningful world
  - Not consciousness confronting objects, but engaged coping
  - The world is not "out there" but constituted through embodied activity
  - Motor intentionality: moving toward possibilities, not representing objects
- **Flesh (*la chair*):** Later ontology of intertwining
  - "Flesh" is neither subject nor object but their common element
  - The seer is also visible; the toucher is touched
  - Chiasm: reversibility of perceiving and perceived
  - The body is "of" the world, not merely "in" it
  - Overcomes subject-object dualism at the ontological level
- **Operative intentionality:** Pre-reflective directedness
  - Beneath explicit judgments lies a deeper, bodily intentionality
  - We engage the world before representing it
  - Practical understanding precedes theoretical knowledge
  - Anticipates embodied cognition in cognitive science

**Key Works:**
- *Phenomenology of Perception* (1945) — major work on embodiment
- *The Structure of Behavior* (1942) — critique of behaviorism
- *The Visible and the Invisible* (1964, posthumous) — later ontology
- *Signs* (1960) — essays on language, politics, philosophy
- *Sense and Non-Sense* (1948) — essays

**Connections:**
- *Influenced by*: Husserl (phenomenology), Heidegger, Gestalt psychology
- *Influences*: Cognitive science, embodied AI, philosophy of mind
- *With*: Sartre, Beauvoir (Les Temps Modernes), later broke with Sartre
- *Against*: Cartesianism, behaviorism, intellectualism

**Common Misconceptions:**
- He wasn't simply "Sartre's colleague"—his philosophy is quite different
- The body-subject isn't behaviorism—it's phenomenological
- "Flesh" isn't physical matter—it's an ontological category
- He didn't reject science—he wanted to integrate it with phenomenology
- His politics evolved; he criticized both capitalism and Stalinism

**Why He Matters:** Merleau-Ponty is the philosopher of embodiment. His phenomenology of perception influenced cognitive science, artificial intelligence, and philosophy of mind. His critique of Cartesian dualism remains powerful. The concept of the body-subject transformed how we understand subjectivity. His later work on "flesh" offers a radical rethinking of the subject-object distinction.

**Future Exploration:**
- How does Merleau-Ponty's embodiment relate to contemporary cognitive science?
- What's the relationship between early and late Merleau-Ponty?
- Can the concept of "flesh" overcome dualism?
- How does his phenomenology differ from Husserl's and Heidegger's?
- What are the political implications of his philosophy?

---

### Structuralism and Post-Structuralism

> *Language, structures, and the decentering of the subject*
